Extracting Answer from: 
The Triangle Inequality says that the sum of the lengths of any two sides must be greater than the length of the third side. That means $8+8=16$ must be greater than the length of the third side. The third side has a whole number length, so the greatest possible length is 15 units. That makes the perimeter $8+8+15=\boxed{31}$ units.

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/geometry/106_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Let $x$ be the length of the third side. By the triangle inequality, we have $8+8>x$ and $8+x>8$, which simplify to $x<16$ and $x>0$. Also, the third side must be shorter than the sum of the other two, so $x<16-8=8$. The largest whole number satisfying $0<x<8$ is $x=7$, so the greatest possible perimeter is $8+8+7=\boxed{23}$ units.

Extracting Answer from: 
We recognize 7, 24, and 25 as a Pythagorean triple. That means the area of the triangle is $\frac{1}{2} \cdot 7 \cdot 24 = 84.$ The altitudes are then easy to find: $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{7},$ $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{24},$ and $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{25}.$ Then, our answer is $\frac{168}{7} + \frac{168}{24} + \frac{168}{25} = \boxed{37.72}.$

Answer from Original: 37.72

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/geometry/1065_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
The altitudes of the triangle can be calculated using the formula $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$, where $A$ is the area of the triangle, $b$ is the length of the corresponding base, and $h$ is the length of the corresponding altitude. Using Heron's formula to find the area of the triangle, we get:

\begin{align*}
s &= \frac{a+b+c}{2} \\
&= \frac{7+24+25}{2} \\
&= 28 \\
\end{align*}

\begin{align*}
A &= \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)} \\
&= \sqrt{28(28-7)(28-24)(28-25)} \\
&= 84 \\
\end{align*}

Now we can use the formula $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$ to find the length of each altitude. Let $h_1$ be the altitude from the side of length $7$, $h_2$ be the altitude from the side of length $24$, and $h_3$ be the altitude from the side of length $25$. Then:

\begin{align*}
h_1 &= \frac{2A}{7} \\
&= \frac{2\cdot 84}{7} \\
&= 24 \\
\end{align*}

\begin{align*}
h_2 &= \frac{2A}{24} \\
&= \frac{2\cdot 84}{24} \\
&= 7 \\
\end{align*}

\begin{align*}
h_3 &= \frac{2A}{25} \\
&= \frac{2\cdot 84}{25} \\
&= 6.72 \\
\end{align*}

Therefore, the sum of the lengths of the three altitudes is $24+7+6.72 = \boxed{37.72}$ units.

Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/geometry/1077.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Since the measure of angle $BAC$ is 42, the other two equal angles of triangle $ABC$ must be $(180 - 42)/2 = 138/2 = 69$ degrees each. We will introduce the point $F$, which is the center of the circle, and draw segments from each vertex to $F$. Since segment $ED$ is tangent to the circle at point $C$, it must be perpendicular to radius $CF$. Angle $BAC$ is bisected by segment $AF$, so angle $FAC$ is 21 degrees. Angle $FCA$ is also 21 degrees since triangle $AFC$ is isosceles. Thus, the measure of angle $ACD$ is $90 - 21 = \boxed{69\text{ degrees}}$, which is the same as the two base angles of triangle ABC. Since $\triangle ABC$ is isosceles with $AB=AC$, angle $\angle BAC=42^{\circ}$ implies that angles $\angle CBA = \frac{1}{2}(180^{\circ}-42^{\circ})=69^{\circ}$ and $\angle BAC = 69^{\circ}$. Because triangle $ABC$ is inscribed in a circle, $\angle ACB = 69^{\circ}$ as well. Note that $CE$ is a tangent to the circle, so $\angle ECB = 90^{\circ}$. Therefore, $\angle ACD = \angle ACB - \angle BCD = 69^{\circ} - 90^{\circ} = \boxed{-21^{\circ}}$.

A circular sheet of paper with radius 6 inches has a circumference of $2\pi r = 2 \pi (6) = 12\pi$ inches. Since this sheet of paper is cut into 3 equal sectors, each sector must have an arc length of $1/3$ that of the original circle, so each sector has an arc length of $4\pi$ inches. Because of the way that a cone is formed, the original radius of the circle now becomes the slant height of the cone and the arc length becomes the perimeter of the cone's base. If we let the radius of the cone base be $R$, then we know that the circumference $4\pi$ must equal $2\pi R$, so $2 \pi R = 4 \pi$. Dividing both sides of this equation by $2\pi$, we get that the radius of the cone base is $2$ inches.

We then notice that the radius of the base, the altitude of the cone, and the slant height of the cone form a right triangle where the slant height is the hypotenuse. Since we know both the radius and the slant height, we can use the Pythagorean Theorem to find the height $h$ of the cone. We have that $2^2 + h^2 = 6^2$, so $h^2=32$. Taking the square root of both sides, we have that $h=\sqrt{32}=\boxed{4\sqrt{2}}$ inches.

Extracting Answer from: 
When the circular sheet is cut into three equal sectors, each sector has a central angle measure of 120 degrees. Then, each sector can be formed into a cone by aligning the two straight sides and connecting the remaining edges. The slant height of each cone can be found by using the Pythagorean theorem, where the radius (6 inches) is the hypotenuse and the height and slant height are the other two sides. 

The slant height, denoted by L, can be calculated as follows:  
L = sqrt(r^2 + h^2)   

where r = 6 inches (radius of the circular sheet) and h = height of the cone. 

To find the height of the cone, we need to use the formula for the volume of a cone. Since each of the three cones has an equal volume, we only need to find the volume of one of them. 

The formula for the volume of a cone is:  
V = (1/3) * pi * r^2 * h  

Substituting r = 6 inches and L = sqrt(r^2 + h^2) in terms of h, we get: 

L = sqrt(6^2 + h^2)  

L^2 = 6^2 + h^2  

h^2 = L^2 - 6^2  

Substituting h^2 in the volume formula, we get: 

V = (1/3) * pi * 6^2 * sqrt(L^2 - 6^2)  

V = 12 * pi * sqrt(L^2 - 36)  

Since each cone has an equal volume, we have: 

12 * pi * sqrt(L^2 - 36) = 3 * V  

Simplifying, we get: 

4 * pi * sqrt(L^2 - 36) = V  

Substituting the given radius (r = 6 inches) and sector angle (120 degrees = 1/3 of a full circle), we can find the volume of the entire circular sheet: 

V = (1/3) * pi * r^2 * h * 3  

V = pi * r^2 * h  

V = pi * 6^2 * h  

V = 36pih  

Substituting this value in the previous equation, we get: 

4 * pi * sqrt(L^2 - 36) = 36pih  

Simplifying, we get: 

sqrt(L^2 - 36) = 9h  

Squaring both sides: 

L^2 - 36 = 81h^2  

L^2 = 81h^2 + 36  

Substituting L^2 = 6^2 + h^2, we get: 

6^2 + h^2 = 81h^2 + 36  

Simplifying, we get: 

80h^2 = 27  

h^2 = 27/80  

h = sqrt(27/80)  

h ≈ 0.658 inches (rounded to three decimal places) 

Therefore, the height of the cone is approximately 0.658 inches.

The formula for the volume of a cylinder is $V = \pi r^2h$, where $r$ is the radius of the base of the cylinder and $h$ is the height. 

We are given that $V = 2700\pi$ and $r = 30$. Substituting these values into the formula, we get:

2700\pi &= \pi (30^2) h\\
2700 &= 900h \\
3 &= h
\end{align*}

Therefore, the height of the tank is $\boxed{3}$ meters.

Since $E$ is the midpoint of $BC$, $BE=EC$. Since triangles $\triangle ABE$ and $\triangle AEC$ have equal base length and share the same height, they have the same area.

Extracting Answer from: 
First, let's assign some variables. Let the width of the rectangle be $w$. Then, since it is four times as long as it is wide, the length of the rectangle is $4w$.

Now, let's examine the shaded region. It is made up of two congruent right triangles, one on top and one on bottom, and a rectangle in the middle. The height of each right triangle is the same as the width of the rectangle, $w$. The base of each right triangle is half the length of the rectangle, or $2w$. Thus, the area of each triangle is $(1/2)bh = (1/2)(w)(2w) = w^2$. The area of both triangles combined is $2w^2$.

The rectangle in the middle has height equal to the width of the rectangle, or $w$, and length equal to the distance between the midpoints of the sides of the rectangle, which is also $w$. Thus, the area of the rectangle is $w^2$.

Adding the areas of the two triangles and the rectangle, the total shaded area is $2w^2 + w^2 = 3w^2$.

The area of the entire rectangle is $lw = w(4w) = 4w^2$. To express the shaded area as a percentage of the total area, we divide the shaded area by the total area and multiply by 100:

$$\frac{3w^2}{4w^2} \cdot 100\% = \frac{3}{4} \cdot 100\% = \boxed{75\%}$$

Therefore, 75% of the rectangle is shaded.

Reflecting $(0,0)$ over the line $x=1$ gives us $(2,0)$ (since the distance from $(0,0)$ to the line is 1 unit, and the image is 1 unit to the right of the line).

Reflecting $(2,0)$ over the line $y=2$ gives us $(2,4)$ (since the distance from $(2,0)$ to the line is 2 units, and the image is 2 units above the line).

Therefore, the resulting point is $\boxed{(2,4)}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
For $\triangle ABC$ to be acute, all angles must be acute.

For $\angle A$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie above the line passing through $A$ and perpendicular to $\overline{AB}$. The segment of that line in the first quadrant lies between $P(4,0)$ and $Q(0, 4)$.

For $\angle B$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie below the line through $B$ and perpendicular to $\overline{AB}$. The segment of that line in the first quadrant lies between $S(14,0)$ and $T(0, 14)$.

For $\angle C$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie outside the circle $U$ that has $\overline{AB}$ as a diameter.

Let $O$ denote the origin. Region $R$, shaded below, has area equal to \begin{align*}

\pi\left(\frac{\sqrt{50}}{2}\right)^{\hspace{-3pt}2}\\
&= \boxed{90 - \frac{25}{2}\pi}.

We "complete" the truncated cone by adding a smaller, similar cone atop the cut, forming a large cone.  We don't know the height of the small cone, so call it $x$.  Since the small and large cone are similar, we have $x/4=(x+6)/8$; solving yields $x=6$.  Hence the small cone has radius 4, height 6, and volume $(1/3)\pi(4^2)(6)=32\pi$ and the large cone has radius 8, height 12, and volume $(1/3)\pi(8^2)(12)=256\pi$.  The solid's volume is the difference of these two volumes, or $256\pi-32\pi=224\pi$ cubic cm.  Thus we see $n=\boxed{224}$.

Since $BE$ is a median of triangle $ABC,$ $BE$ is half of $AC.$  Thus, the line through $G$ that is parallel to $BC$ passes through the midpoint $M$ of $\overline{AB}$ and the midpoint $N$ of $\overline{AC}.$

Since $BC$ is parallel to $MN,$
\[\frac{[\triangle ABM]}{[\triangle ABC]} = \frac{AM}{AC} = \frac{1}{2},\]so
\[[\triangle ABM] = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{1}{3} [\triangle ABC] = 24.\]Then $[\triangle ACN] = [\triangle ABM] = 24,$ and
\[[\triangle ENG] = [\triangle AEN] - [\triangle ANG] - [\triangle EGN].\]Noting that $[\triangle AEN] = [\triangle ABC] = 144$ and $[\triangle ANG] = [\triangle EGN]$ (since they have the same height and the same base),
\[[\triangle ENG] = 144 - 2 [\triangle ANG].\]But triangles $AFG$ and $BDG$ are also each $\frac{2}{3}$ of triangle $ABC,$ so $[\triangle ANG] = \frac{1}{3} [\triangle ABC] = 48,$ and
\[[\triangle ENG] = 144 - 2 \cdot 48 = \boxed{48}.\]

The diameter of the sphere is the same length as a side length of the cube, so the sphere is inscribed in the cube.  The radius of the sphere is half the length of the cube's side: \[r=\tfrac12\cdot30=15.\]The volume of the cube is $30^3=27,\!000$ cubic centimeters, while the volume of the sphere is given by \[\tfrac43\pi r^3=\tfrac43\pi\cdot15^3=9,\!000\pi.\]The unoccupied volume of the box is then $27,\!000-9,\!000\pi$, which expresses the answer in terms of $\pi$. GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/geometry/186_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Since point $(a, b)$ is reflected over the $y$-axis to point $(j, k)$, we know that they lie on a line that is parallel to the y-axis. Therefore, $a = -j$ and $j = -a$. We also know that $b = -k$ because of the reflection over the y-axis. Substituting $-a$ for $j$ in the equation $a + j =0$ gives us $a - a = 0$ which is true. Substituting $-k$ for $b$ in the equation $b + k =0$ gives us $b - b =0$ which is also true. Therefore, we can solve: 

\begin{align*}
a+j &= 0 \\
a+(-a) &= 0 \\
j &= -a \\
\\
b+k &= 0 \\
b+(-b) &= 0 \\
k &= -b \\
\end{align*}

We know that $a + j = 0$, therefore $a+(-a)=0$ and $-a=a$. So $j=-a$. We substitute this equation into $b+k=0$, so we have $b+(-b)=0$ and $-b=k$. Therefore $k=b$. Since $b+k=0$, we know that $2b=0$, or $b=\boxed{0}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
If the edge length of the cube is $s$, then its surface area is $6s^2$ while its volume is $s^3$.  Thus, we have the equation $6s^2 = s^3 + 5s$.  Rearranging gives $s^3 - 6s^2 + 5s = 0$, which factors as $s(s-1)(s-5)=0$.  Thus, the possible values of $s$ are 0, 1, and 5.  As $s$ must be positive, our answer is $1+5=\boxed{6}$.

Note that we need to check that these values of $s$ satisfy the original equation.  When $s=0$, the equation is clearly false.  When $s=1$, we have $6=1+5$, which works.  When $s=5$, we have $150=125+25$, which also works.

We can use the triangle inequality theorem which states that the sum of any two sides of a triangle must be greater than the remaining side. In this case, we have sides measuring 1 unit and 3 units, which means that the third side must be greater than 2 units (1 + 3). Also, the third side must be less than 4 units (3 - 1). Since the length of the third side is an integer value of units, the only possible value is 3. Therefore, the length of the third side is 3 units.

$\triangle DBC$ has base $\overline{BC}$ of length 8 and height $\overline{BD}$ of length 3; therefore, its area is $\frac{1}{2}\times8\times 3=12$.

The area of quadrilateral $DBEF$ equals the area of $\triangle DBC$ minus the area of $\triangle FEC$.

$\triangle FEC$ has base $EC=BC-BE=8-4=4$. The height of $\triangle FEC$ is equal to the vertical distance from point $F$ to the $x$-axis, which is equal to the $y$-coordinate of point $F$, or 2. Therefore, the area of $\triangle FEC$ is $\frac{1}{2}\times4\times 2=4$.

Finally, the area of quadrilateral $DBEF$ is $12-4=\boxed{8}$.

We can find the area of $DBEF$ by finding the areas of triangles $DBF$ and $BFE$ and adding them together.  We can find the coordinates of point $F$ by observing that it lies on the line $y=-\frac{2}{3}x+6$ (since $AD$ has slope $-2$ and goes through $A(0,6)$, we can take a point-slope approach), and it also lies on the line $y=-\frac{3}{4}x+3$ (since $CE$ has slope $-\frac{3}{4}$ and goes through $C(8,0)$, we again take a point-slope approach).  Solving for $x$ and $y$ gives us $F = \left(\frac{8}{3},2\right)$.   

[asy]
size(180); defaultpen(linewidth(.7pt)+fontsize(10pt));
pair A, B, C, D, E, F;
A=(0,6);
B=(0,0);
C=(8,0);
D=(0,3);
E=(4,0);
F=(8/3,2);
draw(E--A--C--D);
draw(B--F--D,dashed);
draw((-1,0)--(10,0), EndArrow);
draw((0,-1)--(0,8), EndArrow);
label("$A(0,6)$", A, W);
label("$B(0,0)$", B, SW);
label("$C(8,0)$", C, S);
label("$D$", D, W);
label("$E$", E, S);
label("$F$", F, SW);
label("$x$", (10,0), dir(0));
label("$y$", (0,8), dir(90));
[/asy]

Since $D$ is the midpoint of $\overline{AB}$, the height of $\triangle DBF$ is half the height of $\triangle ABC$, or 6 units.  The base of $\triangle DBF$ is the distance between $B(0,0)$ and $F\left(\frac{8}{3},2\right)$, or $\sqrt{\left(\frac{8}{3}\right)^2 + 2^2} = \frac{2}{3}\sqrt{73}$.  Thus, the area of $\triangle DBF$ is $\frac{1}{2}(6)\left(\frac{2}{3}\sqrt{73}\right) = 2\sqrt{73}$. 

Similarly, the height of $\triangle BFE$ is the length of $\overline{BE}$, which is half the length of $\overline{BC}$, or 4 units.  The base of $\triangle BFE$ is the distance between $B(0,0)$ and $E(4,0)$, which is 4 units.  Therefore, the area of $\triangle BFE$ is $\frac{1}{2}(4)(4) = 8$. 

Adding the areas of $\triangle DBF$ and $\triangle BFE$ gives the total area of $DBEF$, which is $2\sqrt{73}+8$.  Hence, the area of quadrilateral $DBEF$ is $\boxed{2\sqrt{73}+8}$.

We start by finding the area of $\triangle PRQ$. To do this, we need to know the height of $\triangle PRQ$ with respect to $PR$. Since $\triangle PRQ$ is isosceles with $QR=8$ and $\angle PRQ=120^\circ$, we can use the Law of Cosines to find that $PR=\sqrt{136}$. We can now find the length of $PS$ using the Pythagorean Theorem. We have $$PS=\sqrt{SR^2-RP^2}=\sqrt{(8+24)^2-(\sqrt{136})^2}=4\sqrt{65}.$$

Finally, the area of $\triangle QPS$ is $$\frac{1}{2}(8)(4\sqrt{65})=\boxed{16\sqrt{65}}.$$

Since $\angle WAX = 90^\circ$ regardless of the position of square $ABCD$, then $A$ always lies on the semi-circle with diameter $WX$.

The center of this semi-circle is the midpoint, $M$, of $WX$.

To get from $P$ to $M$, we must go up 4 units and to the left 3 units (since $WX=2$), so $PM^2=3^2+4^2=25$ or $PM=5$.

Since the semi-circle with diameter $WX$ has diameter 2, it has radius 1, so $AM=1$.

So we have $AM=1$ and $MP=5$.

We can proceed by counting. There are $25$ total squares in the square. If we color the $4$ corner squares black, then there is obviously no line of symmetry. If we color the $4$ squares adjacent to the diagonal $\overline{BD}$ black, then there is no line of symmetry either. Finally, if we color the $3$ squares adjacent to the diagonal and also adjacent to a side of the square that is not parallel to $\overline{BD}$, then there will be a line of symmetry on $\overline{BD}$. Therefore, the answer is $\boxed{3}$.

The surface area of a sphere with radius $r$ is $4\pi r^2$.  Thus, Theo's sphere has surface area $4\pi(5^2)=100\pi$ and each of Akshaj's spheres have surface area $4\pi(2^2)=16\pi$, so Akshaj's spheres together have surface area $16\pi\cdot 2 = 32\pi$. GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/geometry/373_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
The surface area of a sphere is given by the formula $4\pi r^2$, where $r$ is the radius. Therefore, the surface area of Theo's sphere is $4\pi \cdot 5^2 = 100\pi$. 

The two spheres that Akshaj has both have radius 2, so each of them has surface area $4\pi \cdot 2^2 = 16\pi$. The total surface area of Akshaj's spheres is then $2 \cdot 16\pi = 32\pi$. 

Therefore, the ratio of the surface area of Theo's sphere to the total surface area of Akshaj's spheres is: 

$$\frac{100\pi}{32\pi} = \frac{25}{8}$$

So the ratio is $\boxed{\frac{25}{8}}$.

We begin by calculating the length of $AB$ and $CD$. Let $E$ and $F$ be the projections from $O$ and $P$, respectively, to line $AD$. Note that $\triangle OEA \sim \triangle OPA$ and $\triangle OFD \sim \triangle OPD$. Thus, we have $EA/PA=OE/OA=2/6$ and $FD/PD=OF/OP=2/4$, so $AD=EA+FD=\frac{1}{3}\cdot PA+\frac{1}{2}\cdot PD$. Also, $EF=OD-OE-PF=6-2-4=0$, so $AB=AD-2\cdot EB=\frac{1}{3}\cdot PA+\frac{1}{2}\cdot PD-2\cdot 2=\frac{1}{3}\cdot 6+\frac{1}{2}\cdot 8-4=\frac{5}{3}$.

Similarly, let $G$ and $H$ be the projections from $O$ and $P$, respectively, to line $BC$. Again we can use similar triangles to get that $BC=GC+DH=\frac{1}{2}\cdot PA+\frac{2}{3}\cdot PD$, and that $CD=BC-2\cdot FC=\frac{1}{2}\cdot PA+\frac{2}{3}\cdot PD-2\cdot 4=\frac{1}{2}\cdot 6+\frac{2}{3}\cdot 8-8=\frac{1}{3}$. 

Now we calculate the area of the hexagon. Splitting it into a rectangle and two triangles yields the formula $[AOBCPD]=[AOBC]+[CODP]=AB\cdot BC+CD\cdot DP=\frac{5}{3}\cdot\frac{1}{2}\cdot 6+\frac{1}{3}\cdot\frac{2}{3}\cdot 8 = \boxed{\frac{46}{9}}$.

Since $QRS$ is a straight line, we have $$40^\circ + \angle QRS = 180^\circ.$$ Since $PRT$ is a straight line as well, we have $$\angle PRT + x^\circ = 180^\circ.$$ In $\triangle PQT$, we have $$\angle TQP + \angle QTP + \angle PQT = 180^\circ.$$ Since $\angle TQP = 40^\circ$ and $\angle QTP = \angle QRS = 140^\circ$, we get $$40^\circ + 140^\circ + \angle PQT = 180^\circ.$$ Simplifying the expression, we get $\angle PQT = 180^\circ - 180^\circ + 40^\circ + 140^\circ = 220^\circ.$

Now, $$\angle QPR = 180^\circ - \angle PQT = 180^\circ - 220^\circ = 40^\circ.$$ In $\triangle QPR$, sum of angles is $180^\circ$, so we get $$\angle QRP = 180^\circ - \angle QPR - \angle QRP = 180^\circ - 40^\circ - x^\circ = 140^\circ - x^\circ.$$ In $\triangle SQR$, sum of angles is $180^\circ$, so we have $$\angle SQR = 180^\circ - \angle QRS - \angle QRP = 180^\circ - 140^\circ + x^\circ - 40^\circ = 40^\circ + x^\circ.$$ 

Finally, in $\triangle STR,$ sum of angles is $180^\circ,$ so we get $$x^\circ + 40^\circ + \angle STR = 180^\circ.$$ Solving for $\angle STR$ and substituting $x^\circ = 100^\circ - \angle STR,$ we get \begin{align*}
& (100^\circ - \angle STR) + 40^\circ + \angle STR= 180^\circ\\
\Rightarrow\ & 140^\circ = (100^\circ + \angle STR)\\
\Rightarrow\ & \angle STR = 40^\circ.
\end{align*} Therefore, $x^\circ = 100^\circ - \angle STR = \boxed{60^\circ}.$

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/geometry/432_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Since the volume of the rubber ball will undergo an increase in proportion to the cube of the radius, we will need more rubber bands for every unit increase in the diameter. We can use the formula for the volume of a sphere to solve for the number of rubber bands needed:

V = (4/3)πr^3

Let's find the radius of the current ball:

d = 3 cm, so r = d/2 = 1.5 cm

Now let's find the current volume of the ball:

V1 = (4/3)π(1.5)^3 cm^3 = 14.14 cm^3

Let's find the new radius of the ball when the diameter is increased by 1 cm:

d = 4 cm, so r = d/2 = 2 cm

Now let's find the new volume of the ball:

V2 = (4/3)π(2)^3 cm^3 = 33.51 cm^3

The difference between the new and old volumes is the amount of additional rubber bands needed, so:

V2 - V1 = (4/3)πr^3 - (4/3)π(1.5)^3 cm^3
V2 - V1 = (4/3)π(2)^3 - (4/3)π(1.5)^3 cm^3
V2 - V1 = 19.38 cm^3 - 14.14 cm^3 = 5.24 cm^3

Since we assume all rubber bands have the same volume, we can divide the difference in the volumes by the volume of one rubber band to get the number of rubber bands needed:

Number of rubber bands = (V2 - V1) / V_rb

We don't know the volume of one rubber band, but we can find it since we know the current ball has 54 rubber bands:

V_rb = V1 / 54 = 14.14 cm^3 / 54 = 0.2624 cm^3 per rubber band

Thus, the number of rubber bands needed to increase the diameter by 1 cm is:

Number of rubber bands = (V2 - V1) / V_rb
Number of rubber bands = 5.24 cm^3 / 0.2624 cm^3 per rubber band
Number of rubber bands = 19.98 ≈ 20

Therefore, Alana should add 20 rubber bands to the ball to increase its diameter by 1 cm.

To find the greatest possible perimeter of an isosceles triangle given two sides $a$ and $b$, we want to set the third side $c$ to be as close to $a+b$ as possible. In this case, we have $a=b=15$ and $c$ can be at most $10+15=25$. Thus, the greatest possible perimeter is $2a+c=2(15)+25=\boxed{55}$ centimeters.

Let the radius be $r$ and the height be $h$. Since the lateral surface area is 3.5 square inches, we have $2\pi rh=3.5$. Since the volume is 3.5 cubic inches, we have $\pi r^2h=3.5$. Dividing the latter equation by the former, we find that $r=\boxed{2}$ inches.

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/geometry/440_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Let the cylinder have radius $r$ and height $h$, so that the lateral surface area of the cylinder is given by $2\pi rh$ and the volume is given by $\pi r^2 h$. Since we are given that the lateral surface area and the volume are both equal to $3.5$ and $\pi$ is a positive constant, we have

$$2rh = 3.5 \qquad\text{and}\qquad \pi r^2 h = 3.5.$$

Dividing the second equation by the first one gives $r = \frac{\pi r^2 h}{2rh} = \frac{\pi r}{2}$, so $r\left(1-\frac{\pi}{2}\right) = 0$. Since $r$ cannot be $0$, we must have $1-\frac{\pi}{2} = 0$, which implies $\boxed{r = 2}$ inches.

Stripped String: 
Letthecylinderhaveradius$r$andheight$h$,sothatthelateralsurfaceareaofthecylinderisgivenby$2\pirh$andthevolumeisgivenby$\pir^2h$.Sincewearegiventhatthelateralsurfaceareaandthevolumearebothequalto$3.5$and$\pi$isapositiveconstant,wehave$$2rh=3.5\qquad\text{and}\qquad\pir^2h=3.5.$$Dividingthesecondequationbythefirstonegives$r=\frac{\pir^2h}{2rh}=\frac{\pir}{2}$,so$r(1-\frac{\pi}{2})=0$.Since$r$cannotbe$0$,wemusthave$1-\frac{\pi}{2}=0$,whichimplies$\boxed{r=2}$inches.

In its initial position, suppose the semi-circle touches the bottom line at $X$, with point $P$ directly above $X$ on the top line. Consider when the semi-circle rocks to the right. [asy]
size(10cm);

// Variables
path semicircle = (-8, 0)--(8, 0){down}..{left}(0, -8){left}..{up}(-8, 0);
real xy = 4 * pi / 3;
pair x = (0, -8); pair p = (0, 4);
pair o = (xy, 0); pair z = (xy, 4); pair y = (xy, -8);

// Drawing
draw((-15, -8)--(15, -8));
draw((-15, 4)--(15, 4));
draw(semicircle, dashed);
draw(x--p, dashed);
draw(shift(xy) * rotate(-30) * semicircle);
draw(z--y);

// labels
label("$Q$", (-4 * sqrt(3) + xy, 4), N);
label("$P$", (0, 4), N);
label("$Z$", (xy, 4), N);
label("$O$", (xy, 0), NE);
label("$X$", (0, -8), S);
label("$Y$", (xy, -8), S);
[/asy] Suppose now the semi-circle touches the bottom line at $Y$ (with $O$ the point on the top of the semi-circle directly above $Y$, and $Z$ the point on the top line directly above $Y$) and touches the top line at $Q$.  Note that $XY=PZ$.

$Q$ is one of the desired points where the semi-circle touches the line above.  Because the diagram is symmetrical, the other point will be the mirror image of $Q$ in line $XP$.  Thus, the required distance is 2 times the length of $PQ$.

Now $PQ=QZ-PZ = QZ-XY$. Since the semi-circle is tangent to the bottom line, and $YO$ is perpendicular to the bottom line and $O$ lies on a diameter, we know that $O$ is the centre of the circle. So $OY=OQ= 8$ cm, since both are radii (or since the centre always lies on a line parallel to the bottom line and a distance of the radius away).

Also, $OZ=4$ cm, since the distance between the two lines is 12 cm. By the Pythagorean Theorem (since $\angle QZO=90^\circ$), then \[ QZ^2 = QO^2 - ZO^2 = 8^2 - 4^2 = 64 - 16 =48\]so $QZ = 4\sqrt{3}$ cm.

Also, since $QZ:ZO = \sqrt{3}:1$, then $\angle QOZ = 60^\circ$.

Thus, the angle from $QO$ to the horizontal is $30^\circ$, so the semi-circle has rocked through an angle of $30^\circ$, ie. has rocked through $\frac{1}{12}$ of a full revolution (if it was a full circle). Thus, the distance of $Y$ from $X$ is $\frac{1}{12}$ of the circumference of what would be the full circle of radius 8, or $XY=\frac{1}{12}(2\pi(8))=\frac{4}{3}\pi$ cm.  (We can think of a wheel turning through $30^\circ$ and the related horizontal distance through which it travels.)

Thus, $PQ = QZ-XY = 4\sqrt{3} - \frac{4}{3}\pi$ cm.

Therefore, the required distance is double this, or $8\sqrt{3}-\frac{8}{3}\pi$ cm or about 5.4788 cm, which is closest to $\boxed{55}$ mm.

We set up a coordinate system with the midpoint of the bottom of the semicircle, called $O,$ at the origin.  Let $A$ and $B$ be the points where the semicircle touches the top line.  Then $A$ and $B$ lie on the line $y = 12,$ and $OA = OB = 8.$  Let $P$ be the projection of $O$ onto the $x$-axis.

[asy]
unitsize(0.4 cm);

draw((-15,-5)--(15,-5),red);
draw((-15,9)--(15,9),red);
draw((-10,0)--(10,0),Arrow(6));
draw((0,-10)--(0,10),Arrow(6));
draw(arc((0,0),8,0,180),red);
draw((-8,0)--(0,0));
draw(rightanglemark((0,0),(0,12),(7.525,4.4),20));
draw((0,0)--(7.525,4.4),dashed);

label("$O$", (0,0), S);
label("$A$", (7.525,12), N);
label("$B$", (-7.525,12), N);
label("$P$", (0,-5), S);
[/asy]

Then by similar triangles, $OP = 12 - 8 = 4,$ and $AP = \sqrt{OA^2 - OP^2} = 4 \sqrt{3}.$

Let $T$ be the midpoint of $\overline{AB}.$  Then $OT = \frac{AB}{2} = 2 \sqrt{64 - 3 \cdot 16} = 8 \sqrt{7/3},$ and $PT = \sqrt{OP^2 + OT^2} = \sqrt{16 + \frac{448}{3}} = 4 \sqrt{13}.$

Therefore, the distance between the two points, in millimeters, is $2PT \cdot 10 = \boxed{1051}$ to the nearest whole number.

Let $P$ be the point on the unit circle that is $45^\circ$ counterclockwise from $(1,0)$, and let $D$ be the foot of the altitude from $P$ to the $x$-axis, as shown below.

[asy]

pair A,C,P,O,D;

draw((0,-1.2)--(0,1.2),p=black+1.2bp,Arrows(0.15cm));

draw((-1.2,0)--(1.2,0),p=black+1.2bp,Arrows(0.15cm));

A = (1,0);

O= (0,0);

label("$x$",(1.2,0),SE);

label("$y$",(0,1.2),NE);

P = rotate(45)*A;

D = foot(P,A,-A);

draw(O--P--D);

draw(rightanglemark(O,D,P,2));

draw(Circle(O,1));

label("$O$",O,SE);

label("$P$",P,NE);

//label("$A$",A,SE);

label("$D$",D,S);

[/asy]

Triangle $POD$ is a 45-45-90 triangle, so $DO = DP = \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$.  Therefore, the coordinates of $P$ are $\left(\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}, \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\right)$, so $\tan 45^\circ = \frac{\sin 45^\circ}{\cos 45^\circ} = \frac{\sqrt{2}/2}{\sqrt{2}/2} = \boxed{1}$.

Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/geometry/493.json
Extracting Answer from: 
We know that $OA$ and $OB$ are each radii of the semi-circle with center $O$. Thus, $OA=OB=OC+CB=32+36=68$. Therefore, $AC=AO+OC=68+32=100$.

The semi-circle with center $K$ has radius $AK=\frac{1}{2}(AC)=\frac{1}{2}(100)=50$.

The shaded area is equal to the area of the largest semi-circle with center $O$, minus the combined areas of the two smaller unshaded semi-circles with centers $K$ and $M$.  The radius of the smaller unshaded circle is $MB=\frac{1}{2}(CB)=\frac{1}{2}(36)=18$.  Therefore, the shaded area equals \begin{align*}
&\frac{1}{2}\pi(OB)^2-\left(\frac{1}{2}\pi(AK)^2+\frac{1}{2}\pi(MB)^2\right)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(68)^2-\left(\frac{1}{2}\pi(50)^2+\frac{1}{2}\pi(18)^2\right)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(68^2-50^2-18^2)\\

&=\boxed{900\pi}
\end{align*}

Extracting Answer from: 
We label the hypotenuse $AB$ with length $x$ and denote side $AC$ by $y$. By the Pythagorean Theorem, $BC=\sqrt{x^2-y^2}$. 
[asy]
pair a=(4,4), b=(0,0), c=(0,4), d=(4,0), o=(2,2);
pair E=(2,0),F=(0,2);
draw(circle(o, 2));
draw(a--c--b--cycle);
draw(E--F);
label("$C$", c, NW);
label("$A$", a, NE);
label("$B$", b, SW);
label("$E$", E, S);
label("$F$", F, W);
[/asy]
Let $E$ and $F$ be the points of tangency of the circle and sides $AC$ and $BC$ respectively. Elementary geometric reasoning gives us that these points of tangency bisect their respective sides. Therefore, $AE=y/2$, $EB=x-y/2$, $BF=\sqrt{x^2-y^2}/2$, and $FC=y/2$.
Thus, the area of isosceles right triangle $ABC$ is $$\frac{1}{2}xy+\frac{1}{2}\cdot (\sqrt{x^2-y^2}/2)\cdot y.$$ We equate this expression with $2\pi$ to get $$\frac{x^2}{\sqrt{x^2-y^2}}=4\pi.$$ Squaring, we obtain that $$x^4-x^2(16\pi^2)=0.$$ Noting that $x>0$, we get $x=4\pi$ from the quadratic formula.

Therefore, the area of isosceles right triangle $ABC$ is $$\frac{1}{2}xy+\frac{1}{2}\cdot (\sqrt{x^2-y^2}/2)\cdot y=8\pi^2+4\pi=\boxed{4\pi(2\pi+1)}.$$

Since $PQRST$ is a regular pentagon, $\angle PQR = \frac{180(5-2)}{5} = 108^\circ$. Also, $\angle QRS = \angle QRT = \frac{180-108}{2} = 36^\circ$. Hence, $\angle QXT = 180 - \angle QRT - \angle QXS = 180 - 36 - 36 = 108^\circ$.

Since $QX = XT$, triangles $QXT$ and $QXS$ are congruent by the Side-Angle-Side (SAS) Congruence Theorem. Therefore, $\angle QXS = \angle QXT = 108^\circ$. 

Finally, $\angle XQS = \angle PQR - \angle QXS = 108^\circ - 108^\circ = \boxed{0^\circ}$.

If two sides have length 6, the remaining side could be $2$ since $6+2>6$ and $6+6>2$.  The remaining side could also be 4 since $6+4>6$ and $6+6>4$.  So, this is two more triangles. The volume of a right circular cone can be found using the formula $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$, where $r$ is the radius of the circle at the base of the cone and $h$ is the height of the cone. We are given that the cup has a three-inch radius and needs to hold $30\pi$ cubic inches of water, so we can set up the equation as follows:

\begin{align*}
30\pi &= \frac{1}{3}\pi (3^2)h \\
30\pi &= 9\pi h\\
h &= \frac{30\pi}{9\pi} \\
h &= \frac{10}{3}
\end{align*}

Therefore, the cup needs to be $\boxed{\frac{10}{3}}$ inches tall to hold $30\pi$ cubic inches of water.

Join $PQ$, $PR$, $PS$, $RQ$, and $RS$. Since the circles with center $Q$, $R$ and $S$ are all tangent to $BC$, then $QR$ and $RS$ are each parallel to $BC$ (as the centres $Q$, $R$ and $S$ are each 1 unit above $BC$). This tells us that $QS$ passes through $R$. When the centers of tangent circles are joined, the line segments formed pass through the associated point of tangency, and so have lengths equal to the sum of the radii of those circles. Therefore, $QR=RS=PR=PS=1+1=2$.

[asy]
size(200);
pair P, Q, R, S;
Q=(0,0);
R=(2,0);
S=(4,0);
P=(3,1.732);
label("Q", Q, SW);
label("R", R, dir(270));
label("S", S, SE);
label("P", P, N);
draw(circle(Q,1), dashed);
draw(circle(P,1), dashed);
draw(circle(R,1), dashed);
draw(circle(S,1), dashed);
draw(P--Q--S--P--R);
[/asy]

Since $PR=PS=RS$, we know $\triangle PRS$ is equilateral, so $\angle PSR=\angle PRS=60^\circ$.  Since $\angle PRS=60^\circ$ and $QRS$ is a straight line, we have $\angle QRP=180^\circ-60^\circ=120^\circ$. Since $QR=RP$, we know $\triangle QRP$ is isosceles, so $$\angle PQR = \frac{1}{2}(180^\circ-120^\circ)= 30^\circ.$$Since $\angle PQS=30^\circ$ and $\angle PSQ=60^\circ$, we have $\angle QPS = 180^\circ - 30^\circ - 60^\circ = 90^\circ$, so $\triangle PQS$ is a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. Thus, the answer is $\boxed{30^\circ}$.

Let $T = PQ \cap RS$. Two tangent lines to a circle from the same point are equal in length, so $PT=QT=RT=ST=1$. Since $\angle QTS + \angle QPS = 90^\circ = \angle RTS + \angle RQS$, we have 
\[\angle PQS = \angle QTS + \angle RQS = (90^\circ - \angle QPS) + (90^\circ - \angle RTS) = \angle PQR + \angle PSR.\]

Let the two different sides of the rectangle be $a$ and $b$. Since the perimeter is 26 units, we have the equation $2a+2b=26\Rightarrow a+b=13$. We can rearrange this equation to get $a=13-b$. We want to minimize the value of $\sqrt{a^2+b^2}$. Substituting in the last equation, we have $\sqrt{(13-b)^2+b^2}=\sqrt{169-26b+2b^2}$. This value is minimized when the quadratic $169-26b+2b^2$ is minimized, which occurs when $b$ (which must be an integer) is as close to $-\frac{-26}{2\cdot2}=6.5$ as possible. So let $b=7$ and $a=13-7=6$. So, the shortest possible diagonal is $\sqrt{7^2+6^2}=\boxed{\sqrt{85}}$.

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/geometry/677_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Let's call the length and the width of the rectangle $l$ and $w$. We know that the perimeter of the rectangle is 26 units, so we can write the equation:

$$2l + 2w = 26$$

Simplifying this equation gives us:

$$l + w = 13$$

We want to minimize the diagonal of the rectangle, so we want to find the rectangle with the smallest diagonal that satisfies the above equation. By the Pythagorean theorem, the diagonal of a rectangle with sides $l$ and $w$ is:

$$\sqrt{l^2 + w^2}$$

We can use the equation $l + w = 13$ to solve for $w$ in terms of $l$:

$$w = 13 - l$$

Substituting this into the equation for the diagonal gives us:

$$\sqrt{l^2 + (13-l)^2}$$

Simplifying this gives:

$$\sqrt{2l^2 - 26l +169}$$

To find the minimum value of this expression, we can take the derivative with respect to $l$ and set it equal to zero:

$$\frac{d}{dl}\sqrt{2l^2 - 26l +169} = \frac{2l-13}{\sqrt{2l^2 - 26l +169}} =0$$

Solving this equation gives us $l=\frac{13}{2}$. Plugging this value of $l$ back into the equation for the diagonal gives us:

$$\sqrt{\left(\frac{13}{2}\right)^2 + \left(\frac{13}{2}\right)^2}$$

Simplifying this gives us:

$$\frac{13\sqrt{2}}{2}$$

So the length of the shortest possible diagonal is $\boxed{\frac{13\sqrt{2}}{2}}$ units.

Let $AB = BC = AC = DE = EF = DF =x$, and let the inradius of $\triangle ABC$ be $r$ and that of $\triangle DEF$ be $s$. Then since $ABC$ is right, we have $r = \frac{AB + BC - AC}{2} = \frac{x}{2}$. Also, the area of $\triangle ABC$ is 15 times the inradius: $15 = rs = \frac{x^2}{4}s$, so $s = \frac{60}{x^2}$. 

Now, consider drawing perpendiculars from the incenter of each triangle $\triangle ABC$ and $\triangle DEF$ in the following diagram: 
[asy]
draw((0,0)--(2,0)--(0,2)--cycle,linewidth(2));
draw((0,0)--(1,0)--(1,1)--(0,1)--(0,0)--cycle,linewidth(2));

label("$s$", (3.833, 0.833));
[/asy]
In $\triangle ABC$, the length of the perpendicular from the incenter to $BC$ is $r$. Therefore in $\triangle AED$, which is similar to $\triangle ABC$, the length of the perpendicular from the incenter to $DE$ must be $r \cdot \frac{DE}{AB} = \frac{1}{2}r$. Thus the side length of the square inscribed in $\triangle DEF$ is equal to $2s - r = 2 \cdot \frac{60}{x^2} - \frac{x}{2}$. Its area is therefore $\left(2 \cdot \frac{60}{x^2} - \frac{x}{2} \right)^2 = \boxed{\frac{1635}{16x^4}(4x^2-15)^2}$ square centimeters.

To save the most rope, we must have $HP$ having minimum length.
For $HP$ to have minimum length, $HP$ must be perpendicular to $CD$. [asy]
pair C, D, H, P;
H=(90,120);
C=(0,0);
D=(140,0);
P=(90,0);
draw(H--C--D--H--P);
label("H", H, N);
label("C", C, SW);
label("D", D, SE);
label("P", P, S);
label("150", (C+H)/2, NW);
label("130", (D+H)/2, NE);
[/asy] (Among other things, we can see from this diagram that sliding $P$ away from the perpendicular position does make $HP$ longer.)
In the diagram, $HC=150$, $HD=130$ and $CD=140$.
Let $HP=x$ and $PD=a$.  Then $CP=140-a$.
By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle HPC$, $x^2 + (140-a)^2 = 150^2$.
By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle HPD$, $x^2+a^2 = 130^2$.
Subtracting the second equation from the first, we obtain \begin{align*}
(140-a)^2 - a^2 & = 150^2 - 130^2 \\
(19600 - 280a+a^2)-a^2 & = 5600 \\
19600 -280a & = 5600 \\
280a & = 14000 \\
a & = 50
\end{align*} Therefore, $x^2 + 90^2 = 150^2$ or $x^2 = 150^2 - 90^2 = 22500 - 8100 = 14400$ so $x =120$.
So the shortest possible rope that we can use is 120 m, which saves $130+150-120 = \boxed{160}$ m of rope.

Let $x$ be the length of $HP$.  Then $CP=140-x$ and $DP=x-10$.  Let $Q$ be the foot of the perpendicular from $P$ to line $AD$.  Then $DQ=140-(x-10)=150-x$ and $CQ=x$.  We want to minimize $HQ=HO - OP$. Note that $HO=\sqrt{x^2+625}$, because $OH$ is the hypotenuse of right triangle $OQH$.  Also, \[OP^2=OQ^2+QP^2=(150-x)^2+(x^2-20x+100).\] Hence, $HQ = \sqrt{x^2+625} - \sqrt{(150-x)^2+(x^2-20x+100)}$.  We can simplify this as follows: \begin{align*}
HQ &= \sqrt{x^2+625} - \sqrt{(150-x)^2+(x^2-20x+100)} \\
&= \sqrt{x^2+625} - \sqrt{(x^2-20x+625)} \\
&= \frac{625}{\sqrt{x^2+625}+\sqrt{x^2-20x+625}}.
\end{align*} It follows that we want to maximize $\sqrt{x^2+625}+\sqrt{x^2-20x+625}$, a sum of two square roots.  Since both quantities are positive, we can minimize the negative of their sum: \[f(x) = -\left(\sqrt{x^2+625}+\sqrt{x^2-20x+625}\right).\] Use the chain rule to calculate $f'(x)$.  After some simplification, we get \[f'(x) = \frac{5(2x-25)}{2\sqrt{x^2-20x+625}} + \frac{5(2x+25)}{2\sqrt{x^2+625}}.\] Setting $f'(x) = 0$, we find $4x = 25$, so $x=\tfrac{25}{4}$.  This gives $HQ=\tfrac{625}{30}=\boxed{\tfrac{125}{6}}$ meters.

Extracting Answer from: 
The volume of each of the old containers is given by:

V₁ = πr₁²h

where r₁ = 4 cm (the radius of the old containers) and h is the height (which we assume to be the same for all the containers).

The volume of each of the new containers is given by:

V₂ = πr₂²h

where r₂ = 8 cm (the radius of the new containers) and h is the same height as the old containers.

We need to find out how many of the new containers are required to hold the same amount of sand as the old containers. Since the height of both types of containers is the same, we can set the volumes equal and solve for the number of new containers:

V₁ = V₂

πr₁²h = πr₂²h

r₁² = r₂²/4

r₂ = 2r₁

So each new container has twice the radius of the old containers. Therefore, the number of new containers required is:

12/2 = 6

Sean will require 6 new cylindrical containers with a radius of 8 cm to transfer all the sand from his 12 old cylindrical containers with a radius of 4 cm.

We know that $AG:AD = 2:3$.  Triangles $AMG$ and $ABD$ are similar, so $AM:AB = AG:AD = 2:3$.  Likewise, $AN:AC = AG:AD = 2:3$.

[asy]
import geometry;

unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, G, M, N;

A = (1,3);
B = (0,0);
C = (4,0);
D = (B + C)/2;
E = (C + A)/2;
F = (A + B)/2;
G = (A + B + C)/3;
M = extension(G, G + B - C, A, B);
N = extension(G, G + B - C, A, C);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(A--D);
draw(B--E);
draw(C--F);
draw(M--N);

label("$A$", A, dir(90));
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$G$", G, SSW);
label("$M$", M, NW);
label("$N$", N, NE);
[/asy]

Therefore, the area of triangle $AMN$ is $810 \cdot (2/3)^2 = \boxed{360}$.

First, we find the area of triangle $ABC$ using the median formula.  Let $BC=a$, $AC=b$, and $AB=c$.  Then we have

\[4\cdot 810 = 4[ABC]= 4\cdot \frac{\sqrt{2b^2+2c^2-a^2}\cdot \sqrt{2a^2+2c^2-b^2}\cdot \sqrt{2a^2+2b^2-c^2}}{4}.\]We can simplify this as follows:

\begin{align*}
4\cdot 810 &= \sqrt{(2b^2+2c^2-a^2)(2a^2+2c^2-b^2)(2a^2+2b^2-c^2)} \\
\Rightarrow\qquad 81\cdot5\cdot16 &= \sqrt{(b+c)^2-a^2}\cdot \sqrt{(a+c)^2-b^2} \cdot \sqrt{(a+b)^2-c^2} \\
\Rightarrow\qquad 9\cdot5\cdot16 &= (\sqrt{(b+c)^2-a^2})(b+c)\cdot(\sqrt{(a+c)^2-b^2})(a+c)\cdot(\sqrt{(a+b)^2-c^2})(a+b) \\
\Rightarrow\qquad 720^2 &= (2s-2a)\cdot (2s-2b)\cdot (2s-2c),
\end{align*}
where $s$ is the semiperimeter of triangle $ABC$.  Therefore, $s=\sqrt{3\cdot5\cdot16}=4\sqrt{15}$.

Now, we observe that $\triangle{AGM}$ and $\triangle{AGN}$ are both similar to $\triangle{ABC}$ (due to corresponding angles and parallel lines). Since the ratio $AG:GM$ is 2:1, we have that $[AGM] = \frac{1}{9}[ABC]$. Similarly, we get $[AGN] = \frac{1}{9}[ABC]$. Therefore, $[AMN] = [ABC] - [AGM] - [AGN] = [ABC] - \frac{2}{9}[ABC] = \frac{7}{9}[ABC]$. Therefore, $[AMN] = \frac{7}{9} \cdot 810 = \boxed{630}$.

This triangle's vertices are the intersection points of each pair of lines. The intersection point of $y=0$ and $y=x+4$ is (-4,0). The intersection point of $y=0$ and $x+3y=12$ is (12,0). To find the intersection of the last 2 lines, we substitute the first equation for $y$ and then solve for $x$. Doing so, we get \begin{align*}
x+3y&=12 \\
x+3(x+4)&=12 \\
x+3x+12 &= 12 \\
4x &=0 \\
x &=0
\end{align*} Thus, $y=4$, and the intersection point is (0,4). Let the base of the triangle be the side of the triangle on the $x$-axis. Because this side is between the points (-4,0) and (12,0), its length is $12-(-4)=12+4=16$. The height must be perpendicular to this side and through the final vertex. This is along the $y$-axis. Thus, the height of the triangle is just the $y$-coordinate of the other point, which is 4. Thus, the area of the triangle is $\frac{1}{2} \cdot 16 \cdot 4=8 \cdot 4=\boxed{32} \text{sq units}$.

We could solve for the three vertices of the triangle being formed using the system 
\begin{align*}
y&=x+4 \\
y&=0 \\
x+3y&=12 \\
\end{align*}

Alternatively, we could notice that the horizontal distance from $(0,0)$ to $(6,10/3)$ is 6, and that the vertical distance from $(0,0)$ to $(0,10/3)$ is $10/3$, so the area of the triangle is $\frac{1}{2}(6)\left(\frac{10}{3}\right) = \boxed{10}.$

Let the perpendiculars from $C$ and $D$ to $BE$ intersect $BE$ at $X$ and $Y$, respectively.  These perpendiculars split trapezoid $BCDE$ into two isosceles right triangles $\triangle BCX$ and $\triangle EDY$ and one rectangle $CDYX$.

In isosceles right triangles (which have angles 45-45-90), the ratio of the leg length to the hypotenuse length is $1:\sqrt{2}$; hence, we have $BX=CX=DY=YE=12/\sqrt{2}=6\sqrt{2}$.  We also have $XY=CD=12$, as opposite sides of a rectangle are equal.

Thus, trapezoid $BCDE$ has bases of length $CD=12$ and $BE=6\sqrt{2}+12+6\sqrt{2}=12+12\sqrt{2}$, and height of length $6\sqrt{2}$.  Hence its area is $\frac{1}{2}(12 + 12 + 12\sqrt{2}) (6\sqrt{2}) = \boxed{72 + 72\sqrt{2}}$.

We recall the formula for the area of a trapezoid is $\frac{b_1+b_2}{2}h$, where $b_1$,$b_2$ are the lengths of the two bases, and $h$ is their common height. Our height is $\overline{TC}$, with length $12$. To find the bases, we again utilize our knowledge of 45-45-90 triangles. Let us denote the midpoint of $\overline{CD}$ as $T$. Then $\overline{BT}$ is equal to the altitude of $\triangle ABC$ (because $\overline{BT}$ is an altitude of $ABCD$ and $ABCD$ and $ABCDEFGH$ are similar). By the angle bisector theorem, we have $AX/AB = CX/CB$. Thus, $AX/45=21/(AX+30)$. Multiplying and simplifying yields $$AX^2+30AX-945=0.$$ Using the quadratic formula, we get $$AX=\frac{-30+\sqrt{30^2+4\cdot945}}{2}=\boxed{\frac{15\sqrt{133}}{2}}.$$

First, we look at $\triangle MDN.$ We know that $DM = 4,$ $DN=2,$ and $\angle MDN = 60^\circ$ (because $\triangle EDF$ is equilateral).  Since $DM:DN=2:1$ and the contained angle is $60^\circ,$ $\triangle MDN$ is a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. Therefore, $MN$ is perpendicular to $DF,$ and $MN =\sqrt{3}DN = 2\sqrt{3}.$

Next, we calculate $CP.$ We know that $QC = 8$ and $\angle QCP = 60^\circ.$ Since $MN\perp DF,$ plane $MNPQ$ is perpendicular to plane $BCDF.$ Since $QP || MN$ (they lie in the same plane $MNPQ$ and in parallel planes $ACB$ and $DEF$), $QP \perp CB.$

Therefore, $\triangle QCP$ is right-angled at $P$ and contains a $60^\circ$ angle, so is also a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. It follows that $$CP = \frac{1}{2}(CQ)=\frac{1}{2}(8)=4$$and $QP = \sqrt{3} CP = 4\sqrt{3}.$

Then, we construct. We extend $CD$ downwards and extend $QM$ until it intersects the extension of $CD$ at $R.$ (Note here that the line through $QM$ will intersect the line through $CD$ since they are two non-parallel lines lying in the same plane.) [asy]

[/asy] Finally, we calculate the volume of $QPCDMN.$ The volume of $QPCDMN$ equals the difference between the volume of the triangular -based pyramid $RCQP$ and the volume of the triangular-based pyramid $RDMN.$

We have  \[ [\triangle CPQ]=\frac{1}{2}(CP)(QP)=\frac{1}{2}(4)(4\sqrt{3})=8\sqrt{3}\]and  \[ [\triangle DNM] =\frac{1}{2}(DN)(MN)=\frac{1}{2}(2)(2\sqrt{3})=2\sqrt{3}.\]The volume of a tetrahedron equals one-third times the area of the base times the height. We have $RD=16$ and $RC=32.$ Therefore, the volume of $QPCDMN$ is \[\frac{1}{3}(8\sqrt{3})(32)-\frac{1}{3}(2\sqrt{3})(16)=\frac{256\sqrt{3}}{3} - \frac{32\sqrt{3}}{3}=\boxed{\frac{224\sqrt{3}}{3}}.\]

Let $H$ be the foot of the altitude from $C$ to $AB$. We are given that $CQ = 8$, so $AH = 4\sqrt{3}$.

There are two cases to consider (they turn out to be equivalent):

$\textbf{Case 1:}$ The line passes through $H$.

In this case, the length of the line segment inside each triangle is proportional to the distance from the vertex to the line. Therefore:

\begin{align*}
[QPCDMN] &= \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{2}{3} \cdot QP \cdot CD \cdot AB + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{1}{3} \cdot QP \cdot MN \cdot AB \\
&= \frac{1}{2} AB \cdot QP \cdot (CH + \frac{1}{2}DN + \frac{2}{3}DM) \\
&= 24\sqrt{3} \cdot QP \cdot (\frac{8}{3}).
\end{align*}

$\textbf{Case 2:}$ The line passes through $A$ or $B$.

Without loss of generality, suppose the line passes through $B$ but not $A$. Let $T$ be the foot of the altitude from $Q$ to $BC$, and let $S$ be the foot of the altitude from $P$ to $AB$.

Then $QT = 8$, $BT = 4\sqrt{3}$, and $BS = 12 - 4\sqrt{3}$. Therefore,

\begin{align*}
QP &= QD + DP + PS \\
&= QB + BP + PS \\
&= \sqrt{64 + (12-4\sqrt{3})^2} + \sqrt{4^2 + S^2} + S \\
&= \sqrt{52 + 48\sqrt{3}} + \sqrt{16 + (12-4\sqrt{3})^2} + 12-4\sqrt{3} \\
&= 16.
\end{align*}

Now let $X$ and $Y$ be the feet of the altitudes from $M$ and $N$ to $DE$, respectively.

Then $HX = 8$, $NY = 10$, and

\begin{align*}
CD &= CY + YD = CX + XD \\
&= \sqrt{16 + (16-4\sqrt{3})^2} + \sqrt{16 + 4^2} \\
&= 8\sqrt{3} + 4 \sqrt{7}.
\end{align*}

Therefore,

\begin{align*}
[QPCDMN] &= [QXC] + \frac{1}{2}XY(MN-YX) + [YMD] \\
&= \frac{1}{2} XY \cdot ND + \frac{1}{2} XY \cdot DM + \frac{1}{2} QD \cdot CX \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \cdot 2^2\sqrt{3} + \frac{1}{2} \cdot 4^2\sqrt{3} + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \sqrt{52 + 48\sqrt{3}} \cdot (8\sqrt{3} + 4\sqrt{7}) \\
&= 16\sqrt{3} + 12\sqrt{7} + 28 + 13\sqrt{3} + 6\sqrt{21} \\
&= 41\sqrt{3} + 12\sqrt{7} + 6\sqrt{21}.
\end{align*}

Therefore, the answer is $\boxed{41\sqrt{3} + 12\sqrt{7} + 6\sqrt{21}}$.

For a circle of radius $r$ and an arc of $\theta$ degrees, the arc length is $(2\pi r)\frac{\theta}{360}$. Thus, for the same arc length, the arc angle is inversely proportional to the radius, so the ratio of the radius of circle $A$ to the radius of circle $B$ is $40:55$, or $8:11$. Since the ratio of the areas of two circles is the square of the ratio of their radii, the ratio of the area of circle $A$ to the area of circle $B$ is $(8/11)^2=\boxed{\frac{64}{121}}$.

The length of an arc of $x$ degrees on a circle of radius $r$ is $(x/360)(2\pi r)$.  Let $r_A$ and $r_B$ be the radii of circles $A$ and $B$, respectively.  The length of the $55^\circ$ arc on circle $A$ is $55/360(2\pi r_A)$, and the length of the $40^\circ$ arc on circle $B$ is $40/360(2\pi r_B)$.  Setting these two expressions equal gives $$\frac{55}{360}(2\pi r_A) = \frac{40}{360}(2\pi r_B),$$ which simplifying yields $r_A:r_B = 8:11$.  Therefore, the area of circle $A$ is $(8/11)^2$ times the area of circle $B$, which simplifies to $\boxed{\frac{64}{121}}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
We can use the angle bisector theorem to find that $BD = 24/7$ and $DC = 16/7.$ This allows us to use the Law of Cosines on $\triangle ABD$ and $\triangle ADC:$

\begin{align*}
AD^2 &= AB^2 + BD^2 - 2 \cdot AB \cdot BD \cos(\angle ABD) \\
&= 6^2 + \left(\frac{24}{7}\right)^2 - 2 \cdot 6 \cdot \frac{24}{7} \cdot \cos(\angle ABD) \\
AD^2 &= AC^2 + DC^2 - 2 \cdot AC \cdot DC \cos(\angle ACD) \\
&= 10^2 + \left(\frac{16}{7}\right)^2 - 2 \cdot 10 \cdot \frac{16}{7} \cdot \cos(\angle ACD) \\
\end{align*}

Since $\angle ABD = \angle ACD,$ we can solve for $\cos(\angle ABD)$ in terms of $AD$ and substitute this into the second equation to get rid of $\cos(\angle ACD).$ We end up with the equation:

$$\frac{16}{7} - \frac{48}{7} \cdot \frac{AD^2 + 13}{AD^2 + 49} = 64 - \frac{120}{7} \cdot \frac{AD^2 + 36}{AD^2 + 49}$$

This simplifies to:

$$1936AD^4 - 29120AD^2 + 110880 = 0$$

We can factor to get:

$$16(11AD^2 - 180)^2 = 0$$

Thus, $AD^2 = \boxed{\frac{180}{11}}.$

Triangles $AXC$ and $ABC$ share an altitude from $C$, so \[\frac{[AXC]}{[ABC]} = \frac{AX}{AB} =\frac47.\] Therefore, $[AXC] = \frac47[ABC]$.

Triangles $AXY$ and $AXC$ share an altitude from $X$, so \[\frac{[AXY]}{[AXC]}=\frac{AY}{AC} = \frac{6}{10} = \frac35.\] Therefore, $[AXY] = \frac35[AXC]$, so \[[AXY] = \frac35[AXC] = \frac35\cdot \frac47[ABC] = \frac{12}{35}[ABC],\] which means that the desired ratio is $\boxed{\frac{12}{35}}$.

We can use the fact that the ratio of the areas of two triangles that share a height is the same as the ratio of the lengths of their bases. Let $h$ be the height from $A$ to $BC$.

[asy]
pair A,B,C,X,Y;
A=(0,0); B=(-2.36,3.41); C=(5,0); Y=(3,0); X=(0,-1.72);
draw(A--B--C--cycle); draw(A--X); draw(A--Y);
label("$A$",A,SW); label("$B$",B,NW); label("$C$",C,SE); label("$X$",X,S); label("$Y$",Y,S);
label("$h$",(0,1)); label("$7-x$",(B+A)/2,NW); label("$x$",(A+X)/2,E); label("$10-y$",(C+A)/2,NE); label("$y$",(A+Y)/2,W);
[/asy]

Then the ratio of the area of $\triangle AXY$ to that of $\triangle ABC$ is \begin{align*}
\frac{[\triangle AXY]}{[\triangle ABC]}&=\frac{\frac12(4+y)(h)}{\frac12(7+10)(h)} \\
&=\frac{4+y}{17} \\
&=\frac{10-x}{7}.
\end{align*} Note that $x=AX=4$ and $y=AY=6$. Thus, the ratio of the area of $\triangle AXY$ to that of $\triangle ABC$ is $\boxed{\frac{2}{5}}$.

Let the radius of the large semicircle be $6x$.  The diameter of the smallest semicircle is $\frac{1}{1+2+3} = \frac16$ of the diameter of the largest semicircle, so the radius of the smallest semicircle is $x$.  Similarly, the radius of the next smallest semicircle is $2x$, and the radius of the next semicircle is $3x$.  The unshaded area then is the sum of the areas of the three smallest semicircles: \[\frac12(x)^2\pi + \frac12 (2x)^2 \pi + \frac12(3x)^2\pi = \frac12(x^2 + 4x^2 + 9x^2)\pi = (7x^2)\pi.\] The largest semicircle has area $\frac12(6x)^2\pi = 18x^2\pi$, so the shaded area is \[18x^2\pi - 7x^2 \pi = 11x^2\pi.\] Therefore, the desired ratio is \[\frac{11x^2\pi}{7x^2\pi} = \boxed{\frac{11}{7}}.\]

We know where points $A$, $B$, and $C$ are, and we know that the shape is a parallelogram, so we know that the differences in the x-values and y-values between $B$ and $A$ must be the same as the differences in x-values and y-values between $C$ and $D$.

The solution is $\boxed{(-5, -4)}$, which keeps the vertices in alphabetical order, as shown in the first figure. Two other parallelograms are shown in figures 2 and 3 (where we number them from left to right), but they are not solutions because the points aren't in the correct order. They would be parallelograms $ACBD$ and $ABDC$, respectively. [asy]

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/geometry/905_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Since $ABCD$ is a parallelogram, we know that the opposite sides are parallel. Therefore, the vector pointing from $A$ to $B$ is the same as the vector pointing from $D$ to $C$. Likewise, the vector pointing from $A$ to $D$ is the same as the vector pointing from $B$ to $C$. 

The vector pointing from $A$ to $B$ is $\begin{pmatrix}7 - (-3)\\12 - 5\end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix}10\\7\end{pmatrix}$. The vector pointing from $D$ to $C$ is therefore also $\begin{pmatrix}10\\7\end{pmatrix}$. 

We also have that the vector pointing from $A$ to $D$ is $\begin{pmatrix}x_D - (-3)\\y_D - 5\end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix}x_D + 3\\y_D - 5\end{pmatrix}$ and that the vector pointing from $B$ to $C$ is $\begin{pmatrix}5 - 7\\3 - 12\end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix}-2\\-9\end{pmatrix}$. The vector pointing from $A$ to $D$ is therefore also $\begin{pmatrix}-2\\-9\end{pmatrix}$. 

We can set these two vectors equal to each other and solve for $x_D$ and $y_D$: 
$$\begin{pmatrix}x_D + 3\\y_D - 5\end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix}-2\\-9\end{pmatrix}$$
$$\Rightarrow x_D + 3 = -2 \text{ and } y_D - 5 = -9$$
$$\Rightarrow (x_D, y_D) = (-5, -4)$$

Therefore, the coordinates of point $D$ are $(-5,-4)$.

Extracting Answer from: 
First, we note that $240^\circ = 180^\circ + 60^\circ$. Therefore, we can use the tangent addition formula:
\[\tan 240^\circ = \tan (180^\circ + 60^\circ) = \frac{\tan 180^\circ + \tan 60^\circ}{1 - \tan 180^\circ \tan 60^\circ}.\]Since $\tan 180^\circ = 0$ and $\tan 60^\circ = \sqrt{3},$ this reduces to
\[\tan 240^\circ = \frac{0 + \sqrt{3}}{1 - 0 \cdot \sqrt{3}} = \boxed{-\sqrt{3}}.\]

Let $r$ be the number of inches in the radius of the can.  The volume of the can is $\pi r^2(\text{height})=6\pi r^2$ cubic inches.  The lateral surface area is $2\pi r (\text{height})=12\pi r$ square inches.  Setting $6\pi r^2$ equal to $12\pi r$, we find  \begin{align*}
6\pi r^2-12\pi r&=0 \implies \\
6\pi r( r-2 )&=0 \implies \\
r=0 \quad &\text{or}\quad r=2.
\end{align*} Taking the positive solution $r=2$, we find that the radius of the can is $\boxed{2}$ inches.

Let the radius of the cylindrical can be r inches.

Now, the volume of the can can be given by πr²h, where h is the height of the can.

Therefore, the volume of the can is πr²(6) cubic inches.

Also, the lateral surface area of the can is given by 2πrh square inches.

Since the label covers the entire lateral surface, the area of the label is 2πrh square inches.

But we know that the volume of the can is equal to the area of the label.

Therefore, we have πr²(6) = 2πrh.

Simplifying the equation, we have r = 12.

Therefore, the radius of the can is 12 inches.

[asy]

Since $\sin (180^\circ - x) =\sin x$ and $\cos (180^\circ - x) = -\cos x$ for any angle, we have $$\tan(180^\circ - x) =
\frac{\sin(180^\circ - x)}{\cos(180^\circ - x)} = \frac{\sin x}{-\cos x} = -\tan x$$for any angle for which $\tan x$ is defined.  Therefore, $\tan\angle BDC = -\tan\angle BDA$.

From the Pythagorean Theorem, we have $AC = \sqrt{BC^2 - BA^2} = 24$.  Since $D$ is the midpoint of $\overline{AC}$, we have $AD = AC/2 =12$.  Therefore, we have $\tan \angle BDC = -\tan \angle BDA = -\frac{BA}{AD} = \boxed{-\frac{7}{12}}$.

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/geometry/932_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
[asy]
pair A,B,C,D;

A = (0,0);
B = (0,7);
C = (25,0);
D = (12.5,0);

draw(A--B--C--A);
draw(B--D--C);
draw(rightanglemark(B,A,C,18));

label("$B$", B, NW);

label("$D$", D, S);

Triangle $ABC$ is a $7 - 24 - 25$ right triangle, so $\sin A = 7/25$ and $\cos A = 24/25$.  Then $\tan A = \frac{\sin A}{\cos A} = \frac{7}{24}$.

Triangle $BDC$ is also a right triangle, since $\angle BDC = 90^\circ$, and $BD$ is a common side, so triangles $ABD$ and $CBD$ are congruent.  Therefore, $AD = CD = 25/2$.  Then
\[\tan \angle BDC = \tan (180^\circ - \angle ADB) = -\tan \angle ADB.\]By the tangent subtraction formula,
\begin{align*}
\tan \angle ADB &= \frac{\tan A - \tan B}{1 + \tan A \tan B} \\
&= \frac{7/24 - 0}{1 + (7/24)(24/7)} \\
&= \frac{7}{17}.
\end{align*}Therefore, $\tan \angle BDC = -\frac{7}{17}$, so the answer is $\boxed{-\frac{7}{17}}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
The volume of the original cube is 

V = 2 cm x 2 cm x 2 cm = 8 cm^3

The volume of the new cube is V + 19 cm^3. 

Let x be the length of one edge of the new cube. Then, the volume of the new cube is 

V_new = x^3

We can set up an equation: 

x^3 = V + 19

Substituting V = 8, we get: 

x^3 = 27

Taking the cube root of both sides: 

x = 3 cm

Therefore, the length in centimeters of one edge of the new cube is 3 cm.

Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/geometry/965.json
Extracting Answer from: 
There are many ways to draw the diagram; one possibility is shown below.  We know that $\angle BAC \cong \angle CAD\cong \angle BCA$ since $\overline{AC}$ bisects $\angle BAD$ and $\overline{AD} || \overline{BC}$.  Therefore $\triangle BAC$ is isosceles.  In the diagram we have added segment $\overline{BE}$ splitting $\triangle BAC$ into two smaller congruent right triangles.  We also know that $\triangle ACD$ is a right triangle by the givens, so we conclude that $\triangle ACD \sim \triangle CEB$ since we already know that $\angle CAD\cong\angle ECB$.  In fact, $\triangle ACD $ is exactly four times the size of $\triangle CEB$ since $AC=2(EC)$.  If we let $[\triangle CEB]=K$, then $[\triangle AEB]=K$ while $[\triangle ACD]=4K$.  Thus $6K=42$, so $K=7$ and $[\triangle ACD]=4K=\boxed{28}$.

[asy]
import olympiad; import graph; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); dotfactor=4;
int randangle = 50;
draw((-5,0)--(5*dir(randangle))--(5,0)--cycle);
path x1 = (5*Cos(randangle),5*Sin(randangle))--(-10,5*Sin(randangle));
path x2 = (-5,0)--(5dir(2*randangle));

Extracting Answer from: 
Because $\overline{AC}$ bisects $\angle BAD$, we have $$\frac{[\triangle ACD]}{[\triangle ABC]}=\frac{AC}{AB}.$$ Since $\overline{AC}\perp\overline{CD}$ and $\overline{AD}||\overline{BC}$, we have $$\triangle ADC\sim\triangle ACB\quad\text{and}\quad\triangle BAC\sim\triangle ACD.$$ Thus, we have $$\frac{AD}{AB}=\frac{CD}{CB}\quad\text{and}\quad\frac{AC}{AB}=\frac{AD}{AC},$$ which gives $$\frac{[\triangle ACD]}{[\triangle ABC]}=\frac{AC}{AB}=\frac{AD}{AC}=\frac{[ABCD]}{AC\cdot AB}.$$ Since $[ABCD]=42$, we have $$[\triangle ACD]=\frac{42}{AC\cdot AB}.$$ To finish, we need to find $AC$ and $AB$. Applying the Pythagorean theorem to right triangle $\triangle ACD$, we have $$AC=\sqrt{AD^2-CD^2}.$$ Also, using the fact that $\overline{AD}||\overline{BC}$, we have $$\frac{CD}{CB}=\frac{AD}{AB-AD}.$$ Since $[ABCD]=42$, we have $\frac{BC+AD}{2}\cdot AC=42$, so $$AC=\frac{84}{BC+AD}.$$ Solving for $AB$ gives $$AB=AD+\frac{CD\cdot AB}{BC}.$$ Substituting $CD=\sqrt{AD^2-AC^2}$ gives $$AB=AD+\frac{AB}{BC}\sqrt{AD^2-AC^2}.$$ Solving for $AB$ gives $$AB=\frac{AD^2+AC^2}{AD-AC}.$$ Thus, we have $$[\triangle ACD]=\frac{42}{AC\cdot AB}=\frac{42(AD-AC)}{(AD^2+AC^2)\sqrt{AD^2-AC^2}}.$$ Substituting $AC=\frac{84}{BC+AD}$ and simplifying gives $\boxed{\frac{14\sqrt{3}}{3}}$.

Since $K$ is the center of the semi-circle with diameter $AB$, it follows that $KD = \frac{1}{2}AB = \frac{1}{2}(BC + 2CO) = \frac{1}{2}(36 + 2 \cdot 32) = 100$. Also, note that $OK = OD$ is the radius of the semi-circle, $KD = 100$, and $OD = OC - CD = 32 - 18 = 14$. Thus, we can use the Pythagorean Theorem to solve for the radius of the semi-circle with center $K$:

\begin{align*}
&OK^2 = OD^2 + KD^2 \\
&\implies r^2 = 14^2 + 100^2 \\
&\implies r = \sqrt{14^2 + 100^2} \\
&\implies r = 2\sqrt{566} \\
\end{align*}

The area of this semi-circle is then:

\begin{align*}
&\frac{1}{2}\pi r^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{2}\pi \cdot (2\sqrt{566})^2 \\
&= \boxed{566\pi} \\
\end{align*}

Angles $BAC$ and $BCA$ are each inscribed angles, so each one is equal to half of the measure of the arc they subtend. Therefore, the measures of arcs $AB$ and $BC$ are each 70 degrees, and together, the measure of arc $ABC$ is 140 degrees. Notice that angle $CDA$ is also an inscribed angle, and it subtends arc $ABC$, so $m\angle CDA = \frac{1}{2} (\text{arc } ABC) = (1/2)(140) = \boxed{70}$ degrees.

Since $ABC$ is isosceles with $\angle BAC = \angle BCA = 35^\circ$, it follows that $\angle ABC = \angle ACB = 75^\circ$. Thus $\angle ACD = 35^\circ + 75^\circ = 110^\circ$. Since $\angle ACD$ and $\angle ADC$ form a linear pair, $$\angle CDA = 180^\circ - \angle ACD = 180^\circ - 110^\circ = \boxed{70^\circ}.$$
